WE ARE HOME

 

To the man posting anonymous ame reviews of several Little Saigon restaurants, to the same man who came into my parents‚Äô restaurant insulting everything they stand for:

Didn‚Äôt you hear your children begging you to stop? Didn‚Äôt you notice how embarrassed your wife was? Didn‚Äôt you see the stunned look of everyone around you as you left in a fit of rage, without paying?

There‚Äôs a lot I can break down here. There are many assumptions I can make about you and where you come from.

But I won‚Äôt state them here because I don‚Äôt want to be like you.

Here‚Äôs a fact, though:

You clearly don‚Äôt know us.

So let me teach you something.

In Bolsa, everyone you‚Äôve brushed shoulders with—the very people you dismissed—probably knows more suffering than you will fortunately ever know. They saw their beloved country destroyed by colonialism, then civil war. They left everything they knew for the unknown. They left for a chance at freedom, a chance for their family and their future.

One hand would never be sufficient to count their losses. But this loss, I think, has made them the fiercest, strongest people I will ever know.

It saddens me that you don‚Äôt recognize this. It‚Äôs an unfortunate reminder that as much as my community represents the true American Dream—building a foundation out of uncertain hopes and dreams—people like you would rather be ignorant or spread hate than accept this reality.

But your racism has no power here. Your words mean nothing in Little Saigon. So whatever you hoped to accomplish—in person and online—you have failed.

A person close to me—one of the most passionate, talented people I‚Äôm proud to know—once told me that I needed to write about what I really care about. It goes without saying that it‚Äôs this community. It‚Äôs my home. It‚Äôs my family. So it‚Äôs not cool what you‚Äôre doing.

I would like to think that you will learn from this and become a better person.

But again, I can‚Äôt make assumptions.

Thank you,

B·∫£o Nguy·ªÖn

Proud son of Vietnamese immigrants

Chapter 50

Linh

The Mais and the Nguy·ªÖns will never be the best of friends they once were decades ago. Too much history clouds the waters we share. But at least there are fewer words left unsaid. In a spirit of forgiveness, the Lunar New Year passed with ease.

My mom and B·∫£o‚Äôs mom have taken to sharing their homestyle recipes, updating each other with each culinary treat they make at home. I don‚Äôt think Chef L√™ understood what he was getting into when he invited me and B·∫£o and our families to his restaurant. He apologized profusely, saying he meant to do it right after my mural was unveiled, but his son, Philippe, had gotten sick and there wasn‚Äôt enough time.
